      <p>This article provides a brief overview of the <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> systems of the world, sorted by continent. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_care_systems_classification_by_country">Health care systems classification by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Health care systems classification by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries_with_universal_government-funded_health_system">Countries with universal government-funded health system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Countries with universal government-funded health system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In this system (also known as <a href="/wiki/Single-payer_healthcare" title="Single-payer healthcare">single-payer healthcare</a>) government-funded healthcare is available to all citizens regardless of their income or employment status. Some countries may provide healthcare to non-citizen residents, while some may require them to buy private insurance: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r994648498">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries_with_universal_public_insurance_system">Countries with universal public insurance system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Countries with universal public insurance system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In these countries workers have social insurance. Usually government withholds part of their wage, which is divided between employee and employer. People who don't have legal contract of employment and/or can't register as unemployed may be ineligible for free health care: </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries_with_universal_private_health_insurance_system">Countries with universal private health insurance system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Countries with universal private health insurance system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries_with_non-universal_insurance_system">Countries with non-universal insurance system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Countries with non-universal insurance system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algeria">Algeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Algeria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Algeria" title="Healthcare in Algeria">Healthcare in Algeria</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Algeria" title="Health in Algeria">Health in Algeria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>When Algeria gained its independence from France in 1962, there were only around 300 doctors across the whole country and no proper system of healthcare. Over the next few decades, great progress was made in building up the health sector, with the training of doctors and the creation of many health facilities. Today, Algeria has an established network of hospitals (including university hospitals), clinics, medical centres and small health units or dispensaries. While equipment and medicines may not always be the latest available, staffing levels are high and the country has one of the best healthcare systems in Africa. Access to health care is enhanced by the requirement that doctors and dentists work in <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> for at least five years. The government provides <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cape_Verde">Cape Verde</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cape Verde">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Cape_Verde" title="Health in Cape Verde">Health in Cape Verde</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Medical facilities in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a> are limited, and some medicines are in short supply or unavailable. There are hospitals in <a href="/wiki/Praia" title="Praia">Praia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mindelo" title="Mindelo">Mindelo</a>, with smaller medical facilities in other places. The islands of <a href="/wiki/Brava,_Cape_Verde" title="Brava, Cape Verde">Brava</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santo_Ant%C3%A3o,_Cape_Verde" title="Santo Antão, Cape Verde">Santo Antão</a> no longer have functioning airports so air evacuation in the event of a medical emergency is nearly impossible from these two islands. Brava also has limited inter-island ferry service.<sup id="cite_ref-us_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us-83">[83]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eritrea">Eritrea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Eritrea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Eritrea" title="Health in Eritrea">Health in Eritrea</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Eritrea is one of the few countries to be on target to meet its Millennium Development Goal (MDG) targets for health. Researchers at the Overseas Development Institute have identified the high prioritisation of health and education both within the government and amongst Eritreans at home and abroad. Innovative multi-sectoral approaches to health were also identified with the success. About one-third of the population lives in extreme <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, and more than half survives on less than US$1 per day. Health care and welfare resources generally are believed to be poor, although reliable information about conditions is often difficult to obtain. In 2001, the most recent year for which figures are available, the Eritrean government spent 5.7 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> on national health accounts. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) estimated that in 2004 there were only three physicians per 100,000 people in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>. The two-year war with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, coming on the heels of a 30-year struggle for independence, negatively affected the health sector and the general welfare. The rate of prevalence of human immunodeficiency virus/acquired immune deficiency syndrome (<a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>/<a href="/wiki/AIDS" class="mw-redirect" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>), in Eritrea is believed to be at 0.7%(2012)which is reasonably low. In the decade since 1995, impressive results have been achieved in lowering maternal and <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">child mortality</a> rates and in immunizing children against childhood diseases. In 2008 average life expectancy was slightly less than 63 years, according to the WHO. Immunisation and child nutrition has been tackled by working closely with schools in a multi-sectoral approach; the number of children vaccinated against measles almost doubled in seven years, from 40.7% to 78.5% and the underweight prevalence among children decreased by 12% in 1995–2002 (severe underweight prevalence by 28%). This has helped to some small extent even out rural-urban and rich-poor inequity in health. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ethiopia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Ethiopia" title="Healthcare in Ethiopia">Healthcare in Ethiopia</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Ethiopia" title="Health in Ethiopia">Health in Ethiopia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Throughout the 1990s, the government, as part of its reconstruction program, devoted ever-increasing amounts of funding to the social and health sectors, which brought corresponding improvements in school enrollments, adult <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Infant mortality rate">infant mortality rates</a>. These expenditures stagnated or declined during the <a href="/wiki/Eritrean-Ethiopian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Eritrean-Ethiopian War">1998–2000 war with Eritrea</a>, but in the years since, outlays for health have grown steadily. In 2000–2001, the budget allocation for the health sector was approximately US$144 million; health expenditures per capita were estimated at US$4.50, compared with US$10 on average in sub-Saharan Africa. In 2000 the country counted one hospital bed per 4,900 population and more than 27,000 people per primary health care facility. The <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> to population ratio was 1:48,000, the <a href="/wiki/Nurse" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurse">nurse</a> to population ratio, 1:12,000. Overall, there were 20 trained health providers per 100,000 inhabitants. These ratios have since shown some improvement. Health care is disproportionately available in urban centers; in rural areas where the vast majority of the population resides, access to health care varies from limited to nonexistent. As of the end of 2003, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) reported that 4.4 percent of adults were infected with <a href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">human immunodeficiency virus</a>/<a href="/wiki/Acquired_immune_deficiency_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Acquired immune deficiency syndrome">acquired immune deficiency syndrome</a> (HIV/AIDS); other estimates of the rate of infection ranged from a low of 7 percent to a high of 18 percent. Whatever the actual rate, the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Ethiopia" title="HIV/AIDS in Ethiopia">prevalence of HIV/AIDS</a> has contributed to falling life expectancy since the early 1990s. According to the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Ethiopia)" title="Ministry of Health (Ethiopia)">Ministry of Health</a>, one-third of current young adult deaths are AIDS-related. <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a> is widespread, especially among children, as is <a href="/wiki/Food_insecurity" class="mw-redirect" title="Food insecurity">food insecurity</a>. Because of growing population pressure on agricultural and pastoral land, <a href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a>, and severe <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a> that have occurred each decade since the 1970s, per capita <a href="/wiki/Food_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Food production">food production</a> is declining. According to the UN and the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, Ethiopia at present suffers from a structural food deficit such that even in the most productive years, at least 5 million Ethiopians require food relief.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2002 the government embarked on a <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">poverty reduction</a> program that called for outlays in education, health, <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>, and water. A <a href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> campaign for 14 million children has been carried out, and a program to <a href="/wiki/Resettlement_and_villagization_in_Ethiopia" title="Resettlement and villagization in Ethiopia">resettle</a> some 2 million subsistence farmers is underway. In November 2004, the government launched a five-year program to expand primary health care. In January 2005, it began distributing <a href="/wiki/Antiretroviral_drugs" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiretroviral drugs">antiretroviral drugs</a>, hoping to reach up to 30,000 HIV-infected adults.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ghana">Ghana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ghana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Ghana" title="Health in Ghana">Health in Ghana</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, most health care is provided by the government, but hospitals and clinics run by religious groups also play an important role. Some for-profit clinics exist, but they provide less than 2% of health services. Health care is very variable through the country. The major urban centres are well served, but rural areas often have no modern health care. Patients in these areas either rely on traditional medicine or travel great distances for care. In 2005, Ghana spent 6.2% of GDP on health care, or US$30 per capita. Of that, approximately 34% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guinea">Guinea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Guinea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Guinea" title="Health in Guinea">Health in Guinea</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> has been reorganizing its health system since the <a href="/wiki/Bamako_Initiative" title="Bamako Initiative">Bamako Initiative</a> of 1987 formally promoted community-based methods of increasing accessibility of <a href="/wiki/Primary_health_care" title="Primary health care">primary health care</a> to the population, including community ownership and local budgeting, resulting in more efficient and equitable provision of drugs and other essential health care resources.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In June 2011, the Guinean government announced the establishment of an air ticket solidarity levy on all flights taking off from national soil, with funds going to <a href="/wiki/UNITAID" class="mw-redirect" title="UNITAID">UNITAID</a> to support expanded access to treatment for <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>/<a href="/wiki/AIDS" class="mw-redirect" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UNITAID_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNITAID-87">[87]</a></sup> Guinea is among the growing number of countries and development partners using market-based transactions taxes and other <a href="/wiki/Innovative_financing" title="Innovative financing">innovative financing</a> mechanisms to expand financing options for health care in resource-limited settings. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mali">Mali</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mali">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Mali" title="Health in Mali">Health in Mali</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health in <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">world's poorest nations</a>, is greatly affected by <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, and inadequate <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>. Mali's health and development indicators rank among the worst in the world. In 2000 only 62–65 percent of the population was estimated to have access to safe drinking water and only 69 percent to sanitation services of some kind; only 8 percent was estimated to have access to modern sanitation facilities. Only 20 percent of the nation's villages and livestock watering holes had modern water facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-88">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mali is dependent on international development organizations and foreign missionary groups for much of its health care. In 2001 general government expenditures on health constituted 6.8 percent of total general government expenditures and 4.3 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP), totaling only about US$4 per capita at an average exchange rate. Medical facilities in Mali are very limited, especially outside of <a href="/wiki/Bamako" title="Bamako">Bamako</a>, and medicines are in short supply. There were only 5 <a href="/wiki/Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Physicians">physicians</a> per 100,000 inhabitants in the 1990s and 24 hospital beds per 100,000 in 1998. In 1999 only 36 percent of Malians were estimated to have access to health services within a five-kilometer radius.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morocco">Morocco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Morocco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Morocco" title="Health in Morocco">Health in Morocco</a>
      </div> 
      <p>According to the United States government, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> has inadequate numbers of <a href="/wiki/Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Physicians">physicians</a> (0.5 per 1,000 people) and hospital beds (1.0 per 1,000 people) and poor access to <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> (82 percent of the population) and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> (75 percent of the population). The health care system includes 122 hospitals, 2,400 health centers, and 4 university clinics, but they are poorly maintained and lack adequate capacity to meet the demand for medical care. Only 24,000 beds are available for 6 million patients seeking care each year, including 3 million emergency cases. The health budget corresponds to 1.1 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> and 5.5 percent of the central government budget.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated14_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated14-89">[89]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niger">Niger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Niger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Niger" title="Health in Niger">Health in Niger</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health care system of <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> suffers from a chronic lack of resources and a small number of health providers relative to population. Some medicines are in short supply or unavailable. There are government hospitals in <a href="/wiki/Niamey" title="Niamey">Niamey</a> (with three main hospitals in Niamey, including the <i>Hôpital National de Niamey</i> and the <i>Hôpital National De Lamordé</i>), <a href="/wiki/Maradi,_Niger" title="Maradi, Niger">Maradi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tahoua" title="Tahoua">Tahoua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinder" title="Zinder">Zinder</a> and other large cities, with smaller medical clinics in most towns.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Medical facilities are limited in both supplies and staff, with a small government health care system supplemented by private, charitable, religious, and Non-government organisation operated clinics and public health programs (such as <a href="/wiki/Galmi_Hospital" title="Galmi Hospital">Galmi Hospital</a> near Birnin Konni and Maradi). Government hospitals, as well as <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> programmes, fall under the control of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nigerien_Ministry_of_Health&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nigerien Ministry of Health (page does not exist)">Nigerien Ministry of Health</a>. A number of private for profit clinics ("<i>Cabinets Médical Privé</i>") operate in Niamey. The total expenditure on health per capita in 2005 was Intl $25. There were 377 <a href="/wiki/Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Physicians">Physicians</a> in Niger in 2004, a ratio of 0.03 per 10,000 population. In 2003, 89.2 percent of individual expenditures on health care were "out-of-pocket" (paid by the patient).<sup id="cite_ref-who2006_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who2006-91">[91]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nigeria">Nigeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nigeria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Nigeria" title="Healthcare in Nigeria">Healthcare in Nigeria</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Nigeria" title="Health in Nigeria">Health in Nigeria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health care provision in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> is a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/concurrent" class="extiw" title="wikt:concurrent">concurrent</a> responsibility of the three tiers of government in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> However, because Nigeria operates a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a>, private providers of health care have a visible role to play in health care delivery. The <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Nigeria" class="mw-redirect" title="Politics of Nigeria">federal government</a>'s role is mostly limited to coordinating the affairs of the <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">university</a> <a href="/wiki/Teaching_hospital" title="Teaching hospital">teaching hospitals</a>, while the state government manages the various <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">general hospitals</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local government</a> focus on <a href="/wiki/Dispensaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispensaries">dispensaries</a>. The total expenditure on health care as&nbsp;% of GDP is 4.6, while the percentage of federal government expenditure on health care is about 1.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Long_run" class="mw-redirect" title="Long run">long run</a> indicator of the ability of the country to provide food sustenance and avoid <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> is the rate of growth of per capita food production; from 1970 to 1990, the rate for Nigeria was 0.25%.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Though small, the positive rate of per capita may be due to Nigeria's importation of food products. Historically, health insurance in Nigeria can be applied to a few instances: government-paid health care provided and financed for all citizens, health care provided by government through a special health insurance scheme for government employees and private firms entering contracts with private health care providers.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, there are few people who fall within the three instances. In May 1999, the government created the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="National Health Insurance">National Health Insurance</a> Scheme, the scheme encompasses government employees, the organized <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a> and the informal sector. Legislative wise, the scheme also covers children under five, permanently disabled persons and prison inmates. In 2004, the administration of Obasanjo further gave more legislative powers to the scheme with positive amendments to the original 1999 legislative act.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senegal">Senegal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Senegal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Senegal" title="Healthcare in Senegal">Healthcare in Senegal</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Senegal" title="Health in Senegal">Health in Senegal</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The health budget in Senegal has tripled between 1980 and 2000, leading to the Senegalese people leading healthier and longer lives – the life expectancy at birth is approximately 55.34 years for men, 58.09 years for women, and 56.69 years for the entire population. Also, the prevalence rate of AIDS in Senegal is one of the lowest in Africa, at 0.9%. However, large disparities still exist in Senegal's health coverage, with 70% of doctors, and 80% of pharmacists and dentists, living in the nation's capital city, Dakar. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_South_Africa" title="Healthcare in South Africa">Healthcare in South Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_South_Africa" title="Health in South Africa">Health in South Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, parallel private and public systems exist. The public system serves the vast majority of the population, but is chronically underfunded and understaffed. The wealthiest 20% of the population uses the private system and are far better served. This division in substantial ways perpetuates racial inequalities created in the pre-apartheid segregation era and apartheid era of the 20th century. In 2005, South Africa spent 8.7% of GDP on health care, or US$437 per capita. Of that, approximately 42% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sudan">Sudan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sudan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Sudan" title="Health in Sudan">Health in Sudan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Outside urban areas, little health care is available in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, helping account for a relatively low average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> of 57 years and an <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rate of 69 deaths per 1,000 live births, low by standards in Middle Eastern but not African countries. For most of the period since independence in 1956, Sudan has experienced <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a>, which has diverted resources to military use that otherwise might have gone into health care and training of professionals, many of whom have migrated in search of more gainful employment. In 1996 the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimated that there were only 9 doctors per 100,000 people, most of them in regions other than the South. Substantial percentages of the population lack access to safe water and sanitary facilities. <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a> is widespread outside the central Nile corridor because of population displacement from war and from recurrent droughts; these same factors together with a scarcity of medicines make diseases difficult to control. Child immunization against most major childhood diseases, however, had risen to approximately 60 percent by the late 1990s from very low rates in earlier decades. Spending on health care is quite low – only 1 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) in 1998 (latest data). The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> placed the rate of human immunodeficiency virus/acquired immune deficiency syndrome (<a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>) infection in late 2003 at 2.3 percent for adults, quite low by regional standards. The United Nations suggested, however, that the rate could be as high as 7.2 percent. Between 400,000 and 1.3 million adults and children were living with HIV, and AIDS deaths numbered 23,000. As of late 2004, some 4 million persons in the South had been internally displaced and more than 2 million had died or been killed as a result of two decades of war. Comparable figures for <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> were 1.6 million displaced and 70,000 dead since fighting began there in early 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-97">[97]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Zimbabwe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Zimbabwe" title="Health in Zimbabwe">Health in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Zimbabwe now has one of the lowest life expectancies on Earth – 44 for men and 43 for women,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> down from 60 in 1990. The rapid drop has been ascribed mainly to the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Zimbabwe" title="HIV/AIDS in Zimbabwe">HIV/AIDS pandemic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> has risen from 59 per thousand in the late 1990s to 123 per 1000 by 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-nofix_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nofix-99">[99]</a></sup> The health system has more or less collapsed: By the end of November 2008, three of Zimbabwe's four major <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a> had shut down, along with the Zimbabwe <a href="/wiki/Medical_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical School">Medical School</a> and the fourth major hospital had two <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ward" class="extiw" title="wikt:ward">wards</a> and no <a href="/wiki/Operating_theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating theatre">operating theatres</a> working.<sup id="cite_ref-hospitals_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hospitals-100">[100]</a></sup> Due to <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation_in_Zimbabwe" title="Hyperinflation in Zimbabwe">hyperinflation</a>, those hospitals still open are not able to obtain basic drugs and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coping_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coping-101">[101]</a></sup> The ongoing political and economic crisis also contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> of the doctors and people with medical knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Zimbabwe_cholera_deaths_near_500_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimbabwe_cholera_deaths_near_500-102">[102]</a></sup> In August 2008, large areas of Zimbabwe were struck by the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Argentina">Argentina</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Argentina">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Argentina" title="Health care in Argentina">Health care in Argentina</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>’s health care system is composed of three sectors: the <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a>, financed through <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a>, financed through voluntary <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">insurance schemes</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Social_security" class="mw-redirect" title="Social security">social security</a> sector, financed through obligatory insurance schemes. The Ministry of Health and Social Action (MSAS), oversees all three subsectors of the health care system and is responsible for setting of regulation, evaluation and collecting statistics. </p>
      <p>Argentina has three sectors. The public sector is funded and managed by Obras Sociales, umbrella organizations for Argentine worker's unions. There are over 300 Obras Sociales in Argentina, each chapter being organized according to the occupation of the beneficiary. These organizations vary greatly in quality and effectiveness. The top 30 chapters hold 73% of the beneficiaries and 75% of resources for all Obras Sociales schemes and the monthly average a beneficiary receives varies from $5–80 per month.<sup id="cite_ref-idrc_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idrc-103">[103]</a></sup> MSAS has established a Solidarity Redistribution Fund (FSR) to try to address these beneficiary inequities. Only workers employed in the formal sector are covered under Obras Sociales insurance schemes and after <a href="/wiki/Argentine_economic_crisis_(1999%E2%80%932002)" class="mw-redirect" title="Argentine economic crisis (1999–2002)">Argentina's economic crisis of 2001</a>, the number of those covered under these schemes fell slightly (as unemployment increased and employment in the <a href="/wiki/Informal_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Informal sector">informal sector</a> rose). In 1999, there were 8.9 million beneficiaries covered by Obras Sociales.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrientos_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrientos-104">[104]</a></sup> The private health care sector in Argentina is characterized by great heterogeneity and is made up of a great number of fragmented facilities and small networks; it consists of over 200 organizations and covers approximately 2 million Argentines.<sup id="cite_ref-idrc_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idrc-103">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Private insurance often overlaps with other forms of health care coverage, thus it is difficult to estimate the degree to which beneficiaries are dependent on the public and private sectors. According to a 2000 report by the IRBC, foreign <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">competition</a> has increased in Argentina's private sector, with <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a>, American and other <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> <a href="/wiki/Health_care_provider" title="Health care provider">health care providers</a> entering the <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a> in recent years. This has been accompanied by little formal regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-idrc_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idrc-103">[103]</a></sup> The public system serves those not covered by Obras Sociales or private insurance schemes. It also provides <a href="/wiki/Emergency_department" title="Emergency department">emergency services</a>. According to above-mentioned IRBC report, Argentina's public system exhibits serious structural deterioration and managerial inefficiency; a high degree of administrative centralization at the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Argentina" title="Provinces of Argentina">provincial level</a>; rigidity in its staffing structure and labour relationships; no adequate system of incentives; inadequate information systems on which to base decision-making and control; serious deficits in facilities and equipment maintenance; and a system of management ill-suited to its size. The public system is highly decentralized to the provincial level; often <a href="/wiki/Primary_care" title="Primary care">primary care</a> is even under the purview of local townships. Since 2001, the number of Argentines relying on public services has seen an increase. According to 2000 figures, 37.4% of Argentines had no health insurance, 48.8 were covered under Obras Sociales, 8.6% had private insurance, and 3.8% were covered by both Obras Sociales and private insurance schemes.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrientos_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrientos-104">[104]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Brazil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Brazil" title="Healthcare in Brazil">Healthcare in Brazil</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Brazil" title="Health in Brazil">Health in Brazil</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The Brazilian health system is composed of a large, public, government managed system, the SUS (<a href="/wiki/Sistema_%C3%9Anico_de_Sa%C3%BAde" title="Sistema Único de Saúde">Sistema Único de Saúde</a>), which serves the majority of the population completely free of charge or any form of fee, and a private sector, managed by health insurance funds and private entrepreneurs. </p>
      <p>The public health system, SUS, was established in 1988 by the Brazilian Constitution, and sits on 3 basic principles of universality, comprehensiveness and equity. Universality states that all citizens must have access to health care services, without any form of discrimination, regarding skin color, income, social status, gender or any other variable. There is no form of charging or payment in any public hospitals or clinics, either for Brazilian nationals or foreigners.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Government standards state that citizen's health is the result of multiple variables, including employment, income, access to land, sanitation services, access and quality of health services, education, psychic, social and family conditions, and are entitled to full and complete health care, comprising prevention, treatment and rehabilitation. Equity states that health policies should be oriented towards the reduction of inequalities between population groups and individuals, being the most needed the ones for whom policies should be first directed. </p>
      <p>SUS also has guidelines for its implementation, the most peculiar being popular participation, which defines that all policies are to be planned and supervised directly by the population, through local, city, state and national health councils and conferences. </p>
      <p>The level of public spending is particularly high in relation to GDP for a country of Brazil's income level and in comparison with its emerging-market peers. Government outlays on health care alone account for nearly 9% of GDP, the second largest item of spending following social protection. In health care, a number of conventional output indicators are not out of step with OECD averages. Following the decentralization of service delivery in the early 1990s, increasing emphasis has appropriately been placed on enhancing preventive care. But, in a decentralized setting, cost-effectiveness depends a great deal on the ability of service deliverers to exploit economies of scale and scope. Experience with inter-municipal initiatives for procurement, as well as flexible arrangements for hospital administration and human-resource management, is by and large positive. </p>
      <p>Private Health Insurance is widely available in Brazil and may be purchased on an individual-basis or obtained as a work benefit (major employers usually offer private health insurance benefits). Public health care is still accessible for those who choose to obtain private health insurance. As of March 2007, more than 37 million Brazilians had some sort of private health insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Canada">Health care in Canada</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Canada" title="Health in Canada">Health in Canada</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> has a federally sponsored, publicly funded <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(Canada)" title="Medicare (Canada)">Medicare</a> system. Most services are provided by the public sector, and the remainder are provided by the private sector. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Political responsibility
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>The Medicare system spans the federal and provincial governments and funding flows from the federal governments to the provinces to administer. The country's prime minister and most provincial premiers say they are committed to the <a href="/wiki/Social_Union_Framework_Agreement" title="Social Union Framework Agreement">Social Union Framework Agreement</a> that promotes "comprehensiveness, universality, portability, public administration and accessibility" in health care.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Retrieved 20 December 2006. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        GDP
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>In 2005, Canada spent 9.8% of GDP on health care, or US$3,463 per capita. Of that, approximately 70% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Services and costs
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>Canada's system is a <a href="/wiki/Single-payer_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-payer system">single-payer system</a>, where basic services are provided by private doctors. Hospitals provide additional services as well as community health clinics, and private clinics. Costs are paid for by the government for many services, but other services can be covered by private health plans that many employers offer as benefits. Some provinces still charge premiums to individuals and families. As well, some hospitals charge minimal user fees for <a href="/wiki/Emergency_department" title="Emergency department">emergency department</a> visits. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Private sector involvement
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>Other areas of health care, such as <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optometry" title="Optometry">optometry</a>, are wholly private, although in some provinces, emergency visits to optometrists are partly covered by medicare. Some provinces also allow private services which replicate those that are publicly offered, such as diagnostic services such as ultrasounds, x-Rays, and MRI. As well, many private companies acting as benefits or medical insurance providers have flourished. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Doctor billings
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>Billing by doctors is complex and varies from province to province. In 1984, the <a href="/wiki/Canada_Health_Act" title="Canada Health Act">Canada Health Act</a> was passed, which prohibited extra billing by doctors on patients while at the same time billing the public insurance system. Each province may opt out, though none currently choose to. Most family doctors receive a fee per visit. These rates are negotiated between the provincial governments and the province's medical associations, usually on an annual basis. A physician cannot charge a fee for a service that is higher than the negotiated rate – even to patients who are not covered by the publicly funded system – unless he or she opts out of billing the publicly funded system altogether. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Pharmaceuticals
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>Pharmaceutical costs are set at a global median by government price controls. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Improvement of the Canadian healthcare system
       </dt>
      </dl> 
      <p>In May 2011, the Health Council released a report entitled: "Progress Report 2011: Health Care Renewal in Canada", which provides a pan-Canadian look at five key commitments of the 2003 First Ministers' Accord on Health Care Renewal<sup id="cite_ref-2003accord_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2003accord-108">[108]</a></sup> and the 2004 10-Year Plan to Strengthen Health Care.<sup id="cite_ref-10yrplan_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10yrplan-109">[109]</a></sup> This report highlights the progress being achieved to date on wait times, pharmaceuticals management, electronic health records, teletriage, and health innovation. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chile">Chile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Chile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Chile" title="Healthcare in Chile">Healthcare in Chile</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> has maintained a dual health care system in which its citizens can voluntarily opt for coverage by either the public National Health Insurance Fund or any of the country's private health insurance companies. 68% of the population is covered by the public fund and 18% by private companies. The remaining 14% is covered by other not-for-profit agencies or has no specific coverage. The system's duality has led to increasing inequalities prompting the Chilean government to introduce major reforms in health care provision. Chile's health care system is funded by a universal income tax deduction equal to 7% of every worker's wage. Many private health insurance companies encourage people to pay a variable extra on top of the 7% premium to upgrade their basic health plans. Because of this arrangement, the public and private health subsystems have existed almost completely separate from each other rather than coordinating to achieve common health objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia">Colombia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Colombia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Colombia" title="Health care in Colombia">Health care in Colombia</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costa_Rica">Costa Rica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Costa Rica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Costa_Rica" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Costa Rica">Health care in Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> provides universal health care to its citizens and permanent residents. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuba">Cuba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cuba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Cuba" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Cuba">Health care in Cuba</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Cuba" title="Health in Cuba">Health in Cuba</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health care in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> consists of a government-coordinated system that guarantees universal coverage and consumes a lower proportion of the nation's GDP (7.3%) than some highly privatised systems (e.g. USA: 16%) (OECD 2008). The system does charge fees in treating elective treatment for patients from abroad, but tourists who fall ill are treated free in Cuban hospitals. Cuba attracts patients mostly from <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> by offering care of comparable quality to a developed nation but at much lower prices. Cuba's own health indicators are the best in Latin America and surpass those of the US in some respects (infant mortality rates, underweight babies, HIV infection, immunisation rates, doctor per population rates). (UNDP 2006: Tables 6,7,9,10) In 2005, Cuba spent 7.6% of GDP on health care, or US$310 per capita. Of that, approximately 91% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="El_Salvador">El Salvador</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: El Salvador">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_El_Salvador" title="Health in El Salvador">Health in El Salvador</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Healthcare in El Salvador is free at the point of delivery. The public health system, which is regulated by the Ministry of Health and Social Welfare, has 30 public hospitals in the country plus various primary care facilities and 27 basic health care systems. According to the law of El Salvador, all individuals are given basic health services in public health institutions. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Mexico" title="Healthcare in Mexico">Healthcare in Mexico</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:IMSSbuildingDF.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/IMSSbuildingDF.JPG/250px-IMSSbuildingDF.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/IMSSbuildingDF.JPG/375px-IMSSbuildingDF.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/IMSSbuildingDF.JPG/500px-IMSSbuildingDF.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:IMSSbuildingDF.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Central offices of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Social_Security_Institute" title="Mexican Social Security Institute">IMSS</a> in downtown <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg/250px-HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg/375px-HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg/500px-HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:HOSPITAL_LA_RAZA_DEL_I._M._S._S..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Social_Security_Institute" title="Mexican Social Security Institute">IMSS</a> <i>La Raza Medical Center</i>, a typical public hospital in Mexico
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><b>Health care in Mexico</b> is provided via <a href="/wiki/Public" title="Public">public</a> institutions or private entities. Health care delivered through private health care organizations operates entirely on the <a href="/wiki/Free-market" class="mw-redirect" title="Free-market">free-market</a> system (e.g. it is available to those who can afford it). Public health care delivery, on the other hand, is accomplished via an elaborate provisioning and delivery system put in place by the Mexican Federal Government and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Social_Security_Institute" title="Mexican Social Security Institute">Mexican Social Security Institute</a> (IMSS). </p>
      <p>Advances in medicine and increasing health knowledge have increased the life expectancy in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> by an average of 25 years in the last years of the 20th century. Of the 6.6% <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> of government revenue spent on health, this provides only health insurance to 40% of the population who are privately employed. The health care system has three components: the social security institute, governmental services for the un-insured (Seguro Popular), and the private sector that is financed almost completely from out of pocket money. The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Social_Security_Institute" title="Mexican Social Security Institute">IMSS</a>, the largest social institution in Latin America, is the governmental institution responsible of executing the Federal Government's health policy. The number of public hospitals in Mexico has increased 41% in ten years from 1985 to 1995. </p>
      <p>According to the site www.internationalliving.com, health care in Mexico is described as very good to excellent while being highly affordable, with every medium to large city in Mexico having at least one first-rate hospital. In fact, some California insurers sell <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> policies that require members to go to Mexico for health care where costs are 40% lower.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Some of Mexico's top-rate hospitals are internationally accredited.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Americans, particularly those living near the Mexican border, now routinely cross the border into Mexico for <a href="/wiki/Medical_care" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical care">medical care</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-113">[113]</a></sup> Popular specialties include <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">plastic surgery</a>. Mexican dentists often charge 20 to 25 percent of US prices,<sup id="cite_ref-WashPost_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WashPost-114">[114]</a></sup> while other procedures typically cost a third what they would cost in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-113">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paraguay">Paraguay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Paraguay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Paraguay" title="Health in Paraguay">Health in Paraguay</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In terms of major indicators, <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Paraguay" title="Health in Paraguay">health in Paraguay</a> ranks near the median among <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South American</a> countries. In 2003 <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> had a <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">child mortality</a> rate of 1.5 deaths per 1,000 children, ranking it behind <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a> but ahead of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>. The health of Paraguayans living outside urban areas is generally worse than those residing in cities. Many preventable diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Chagas%27_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Chagas' disease">Chagas' disease</a>, run rampant in rural regions. Parasitic and respiratory diseases, which could be controlled with proper medical treatment, drag down Paraguay's overall health. In general, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, lack of proper health care, and poor <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> are the root of many health problems in Paraguay.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated11_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated11-115">[115]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Health care funding from the national government increased gradually throughout the 1980s and 1990s. Spending on health care rose to 1.7 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) in 2000, nearly triple the 0.6 percent of GDP spent in 1989. But during the past decade, improvement in health care has slowed. Paraguay spends less per capita (US$13–20 per year) than most other Latin American countries. A 2001 survey indicated that 27 percent of the population still had no access to medical care, public or private. Private health insurance is very limited, with pre-paid plans making up only 11 percent of private expenditures on health care. Thus, most of the money spent on private health care (about 88 percent) is on a fee-for-service basis, effectively preventing the poor population from seeing private doctors. According to recent estimates, Paraguay has about 117 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a> and 20 <a href="/wiki/Nurse" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurse">nurses</a> per 100,000 population.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peru">Peru</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Peru">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Peru" title="Healthcare in Peru">Healthcare in Peru</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Peru" title="Health in Peru">Health in Peru</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Peruvian citizens can opt between a state-owned healthcare system and various private insurance companies. The country has a life expectancy higher than the global average but it also has a high risk of infection, especially near the jungle and other isolated areas, due to the warm climate that favours the reproduction of various insects and bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The mortality rate of the population has been decreasing steadily since 1990 and now stands at 19 deaths per 1000 live births.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinidad_and_Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Trinidad and Tobago">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Trinidad_and_Tobago" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Trinidad and Tobago">Health care in Trinidad and Tobago</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Health in Trinidad and Tobago">Health in Trinidad and Tobago</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> operates under a two-tier health care system. That is, there is the existence of both private health care facilities and public health care facilities. The Ministry of Health<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> is responsible for leading the health sector. The service provision aspect of public health care has been devolved to newly created entities, the Regional Health Authorities (RHAs). The Ministry of Health is shifting its focus to concentrate on policy development, planning, monitoring and evaluation, regulation, financing and research. Citizens can access government-paid health care at public health care facilities where health insurance is not required. The health care system in the country is universal as almost all citizens utilise the services provided. Some, though, opt for private health care facilities for their ailments. </p>
      <p>Recently, the Government of Trinidad and Tobago has launched CDAP (Chronic Disease Assistance Programme). The Chronic Disease Assistance Programme provides citizens with government-paid prescription drugs and other pharmaceutical items to combat several health conditions. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_the_United_States" title="Health care in the United States">Health care in the United States</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_reform_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care reform in the United States">Health care reform in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health_in_the_United_States" title="Health in the United States">Health in the United States</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:259px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:US_Healthcare_Spending.GIF" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/US_Healthcare_Spending.GIF" decoding="async" width="257" height="610" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="257" data-file-height="610"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         U.S. healthcare spending.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (January 2011)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Percent of GDP. From <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a> Health Data 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The United States currently operates under a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed market">mixed market</a> health care system. Government sources (federal, state, and local) account for 45% of U.S. health care expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Private sources account for the remainder of costs, with 38% of people receiving health coverage through their employers and 17% arising from other private payment such as private insurance and out-of-pocket co-pays. <a href="/wiki/Health_care_reform_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care reform in the United States">Health care reform in the United States</a> usually focuses around three suggested systems, with proposals currently underway to integrate these systems in various ways to provide a number of health care options. First is <a href="/wiki/Single-payer_health_care" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-payer health care">single-payer</a>, a term meant to describe a single agency managing a single system, as found in many other developed countries as well as some states and municipalities within the United States. Second are employer or individual insurance mandates. Finally, there is consumer-driven health, in which systems, consumers, and patients have more control of how they access care. Over the past thirty years, most of the nation's health care has moved from the second model operating with not-for-profit institutions to the third model operating with for-profit institutions. </p>
      <p>In the US, the social and political issues surrounding access to health care have led to vigorous public debate and the almost colloquial use of terms such as health care (medical management of illness), <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> (reimbursement of health care costs), and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> (the collective state and range of health in a population). In the US, 12% to 16% of the citizens do not have health insurance. State boards and the Department of Health regulate inpatient care to reduce the national health care deficit. To tackle the problems of the increasing number of uninsured, and costs associated with the US health care system, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> says he favors the creation of a universal health care system.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> However, this view is not shared across the country (see, for example, quotes from <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> opinion columnist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Factcheck.org" class="mw-redirect" title="Factcheck.org">Factcheck.org</a><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>). </p>
      <p>A few states have taken serious steps toward universal health care coverage, most notably <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, with recent examples being the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_2006_Health_Reform_Statute" class="mw-redirect" title="Massachusetts 2006 Health Reform Statute">Massachusetts 2006 Health Reform Statute</a><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and Connecticut's <a href="/wiki/SustiNet" class="mw-redirect" title="SustiNet">SustiNet</a> plan to provide quality, affordable health care to state residents.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The state of Oregon and the city of San Francisco are both examples of governments that adopted universal healthcare systems for strictly fiscal reasons. </p>
      <p>The United States is alone among developed nations in not having a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system; the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Patient_Protection_and_Affordable_Care_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act">Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act</a> provides a nationwide <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Health insurance exchange">health insurance exchange</a> that came to fruition in 2014, but this is not universal in the way similar countries mean it.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Healthcare in the U.S. does, however, have significant publicly funded components. <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> covers the elderly and disabled with a historical work record, <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a> is available for some, but not all, of the poor,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/State_Children%27s_Health_Insurance_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="State Children's Health Insurance Program">State Children's Health Insurance Program</a> covers children of low-income families. The <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Health_Administration" title="Veterans Health Administration">Veterans Health Administration</a> directly provides health care to U.S. military veterans through a nationwide network of government hospitals; while active duty service members, retired service members and their dependents are eligible for benefits through <a href="/wiki/TRICARE" class="mw-redirect" title="TRICARE">TRICARE</a>. Together, these tax-financed programs cover 27.8% of the population<sup id="cite_ref-Census_2007_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_2007-127">[127]</a></sup> and make the government the largest health insurer in the nation. The U.S. also spends 17.9% of GDP per year on healthcare, more than double the nearest developed nations expenditure. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Venezuela">Venezuela</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Venezuela">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Venezuela" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare in Venezuela">Healthcare in Venezuela</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Venezuela" title="Health in Venezuela">Health in Venezuela</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The right to health care is guaranteed in the Venezuelan Constitution. Government campaigns for the prevention, elimination, and control of major health hazards have been generally successful. Immunization campaigns have systematically improved children's health, and regular campaigns to destroy disease-bearing insects and to improve water and sanitary facilities have all boosted Venezuela's health indicators to some of the highest levels in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The availability of low- or no-cost health care provided by the Venezuelan Institute of Social Security has also made Venezuela's health care infrastructure one of the more advanced in the region. However, despite being the most comprehensive and well funded in the region, the health care system has deteriorated sharply since the 1980s. Government expenditures on health care constituted an estimated 4.1 percent of gross domestic product in 2002. Total health expenditures per capita in 2001 totaled US$386. Per capita government expenditures on health in 2001 totaled US$240. </p>
