      <p>In April 2017 Venezuela's health ministry reported that maternal mortality jumped by 65% in 2016 and that the number of infant deaths rose by 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-129">[129]</a></sup> It also said that the number of cases of malaria was up by 76%.<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-129">[129]</a></sup> The ministry had not reported health data in two years. Venezuela is suffering from acute shortages of food and medicines. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Afghanistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Afghanistan" title="Health in Afghanistan">Health in Afghanistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The citizens of Afghanistan benefited from a well established free universal healthcare system until the arrival of the Mujahideen in 1992; which destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> system of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, forcing most medical professionals to leave the country and causing all medical training programs to cease.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2004 Afghanistan had one medical facility for every 27,000 people, and some centers were responsible for as many as 300,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2004 international organizations provided a large share of medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> An estimated one-quarter of the population had no access to health care.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2003 there were 11 <a href="/wiki/Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Physicians">physicians</a> and 18 <a href="/wiki/Nurses" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurses">nurses</a> per 100,000 population, and the per capita health expenditure was US$28.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhutan">Bhutan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Bhutan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Bhutan" title="Health in Bhutan">Health in Bhutan</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>'s health care system development accelerated in the early 1960s with the establishment of the Department of Public Health and the opening of new hospitals and dispensaries throughout the country. By the early 1990s, health care was provided through twenty-nine general hospitals (including five leprosy hospitals, three army hospitals, and one mobile hospital), forty-six dispensaries, sixty-seven basic health units, four indigenous-medicine dispensaries, and fifteen <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> eradication centers. The major hospitals were the <a href="/wiki/Jigme_Dorji_Wangchuck_National_Referral_Hospital" title="Jigme Dorji Wangchuck National Referral Hospital">National Referral Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thimphu" title="Thimphu">Thimphu</a>, and other hospitals in <a href="/wiki/Geylegphug" class="mw-redirect" title="Geylegphug">Geylegphug</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tashigang" title="Tashigang">Tashigang</a>. Hospital beds in 1988 totaled 932. There was a severe shortage of health care personnel with official statistics reporting only 142 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a> and 678 <a href="/wiki/Paramedic" title="Paramedic">paramedics</a>, about one health care professional for every 2,000 people, or only one physician for almost 10,000 people. Training for health care assistants, nurses' <a href="/wiki/Nurse%27s_aide" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurse's aide">aides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midwive" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwive">midwives</a>, and primary health care workers was provided at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institute_of_Health_Sciences_(Bhutan)" title="Royal Institute of Health Sciences (Bhutan)">Royal Institute of Health Sciences</a>, associated with Thimphu General Hospital, which was established in 1974. Graduates of the school were the core of the national public health system and helped staff the primary care basic health units throughout the country. Additional health care workers were recruited from among volunteers in villages to supplement primary health care.<sup id="cite_ref-cs_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs-130">[130]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Traditional_Medicine_Services_(Bhutan)" title="Institute of Traditional Medicine Services (Bhutan)">Institute of Traditional Medicine Services</a> supports indigenous medical centers associated with the district hospitals. </p> 
      <h3><span id="People.27s_Republic_of_China"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="People's_Republic_of_China">People's Republic of China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: People's Republic of China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_China" title="Healthcare in China">Healthcare in China</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_China" title="Health in China">Health in China</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Hong_Kong" title="Healthcare in Hong Kong">Healthcare in Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Hong_Kong" title="Health in Hong Kong">Health in Hong Kong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Macau" class="mw-redirect" title="Health in Macau">Health in Macau</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The effective public health work in controlling epidemic disease during the early years of the PRC and, after reform began in 1978, the dramatic improvements in nutrition greatly improved the health and life expectancy of the Chinese people. The 2000 WHO <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Report" title="World Health Report"><i>World Health Report – Health systems: improving performance</i></a> found that China's health care system before 1980 performed far better than countries at a comparable level of development, since 1980 ranks much lower than comparable countries.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2000_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2000-131">[131]</a></sup> The end of the famed "<a href="/wiki/Barefoot_doctor" title="Barefoot doctor">barefoot doctor</a>" system was abolished in 1981. </p>
      <p>China is undertaking a <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_system_reform_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare system reform in the People's Republic of China">reform on its health care system</a>. The New Rural Co-operative Medical Care System (NRCMCS) is a new 2005 initiative to overhaul the health care system, particularly intended to make it more affordable for the rural poor. Under the NRCMCS, the annual cost of medical cover is 50 yuan (US$7) per person. Of that, 20 yuan is paid in by the central government, 20 yuan by the provincial government and a contribution of 10 yuan is made by the patient. As of September 2007, around 80% of the whole rural population of China had signed up (about 685 million people). The system is tiered, depending on the location. If patients go to a small hospital or clinic in their local town, the scheme will cover from 70–80% of their bill. If they go to a county one, the percentage of the cost being covered falls to about 60%. And if they need specialist help in a large modern city hospital, they have to bear most of the cost themselves, the scheme would cover about 30% of the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Health care was provided in both rural and urban areas through a three-tiered system. In rural areas the first tier was made up of barefoot doctors working out of village medical centers. They provided preventive and primary-care services, with an average of two doctors per 1,000 people. At the next level were the township health centers, which functioned primarily as out-patient clinics for about 10,000 to 30,000 people each. These centers had about ten to thirty beds each, and the most qualified members of the staff were assistant doctors. The two lower-level tiers made up the "rural collective health system" that provided most of the country's medical care. Only the most seriously ill patients were referred to the third and final tier, the county hospitals, which served 200,000 to 600,000 people each and were staffed by senior doctors who held degrees from 5-year <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">medical schools</a>. Health care in urban areas was provided by paramedical personnel assigned to factories and neighborhood health stations. If more professional care was necessary the patient was sent to a district hospital, and the most serious cases were handled by municipal hospitals. To ensure a higher level of care, a number of state enterprises and government agencies sent their employees directly to district or municipal hospitals, circumventing the paramedical, or barefoot doctor, stage. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_India" title="Healthcare in India">Healthcare in India</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_India" title="Health in India">Health in India</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In India, the hospitals and clinics are run by government, charitable trusts and by private organizations. The public clinics in rural areas are called <a href="/wiki/Primary_Health_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary Health Centre">Primary Health Centres</a> (PHCs). Public healthcare is free for all and entirely funded through taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Major hospitals are located in district headquarters or major cities. Apart from the modern system of medicine, traditional and indigenous medicinal systems like <a href="/wiki/Ayurvedic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayurvedic">Ayurvedic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unani" class="mw-redirect" title="Unani">Unani</a> systems are in practice throughout the country. The modern system of medicine is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Medical_Council_of_India" title="Medical Council of India">Medical Council of India</a>, whereas the alternative systems recognised by Government of India are regulated by the Department of <a href="/wiki/AYUSH" class="mw-redirect" title="AYUSH">AYUSH</a> (an acronym for <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unani" class="mw-redirect" title="Unani">Unani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siddha" title="Siddha">Siddha</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">Homeopathy</a>) under the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_and_Family_Welfare_(India)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (India)">Ministry of Health, Government of India</a>. PHCs are non-existent in most places, due to poor pay and scarcity of resources. Patients generally prefer private health clinics. These days some of the major corporate hospitals are attracting patients from neighboring countries such as <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, countries in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> countries by providing quality treatment at low cost. In 2005, India spent 5% of GDP on health care, or US$36 per capita. Of that, approximately 19% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Indonesia" title="Healthcare in Indonesia">Healthcare in Indonesia</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Indonesia" title="Health in Indonesia">Health in Indonesia</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Community_health" title="Community health">community health</a> system were organized in three tier, on top of the chart is <a href="/wiki/Puskesmas" title="Puskesmas">Community Health Center</a> (Puskesmas), followed by Health Sub-Center on the second level and Village-Level Integrated Post at the third level.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> According to data from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Indonesia)" title="Ministry of Health (Indonesia)">Ministry of Health of Indonesia</a> there are 2454 hospitals around the country, with total of 305,242 bed counting 0.9 bed per 100,000 inhabitant.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wb_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wb-136">[136]</a></sup> Among these 882 of these hospitals are government owned and 1509 are private hospitals. According to the Worldbank data in 2012, there are 0.2 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a> per 1,000 people, with 1.2 Nurses and Midwives per 1,000 people in Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-wb_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wb-136">[136]</a></sup> Out of all the 2454 hospitals in Indonesia, 20 have been accredited by Joint Commission international (JCI) as of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In addition there are 9718 government financed <a href="/wiki/Puskesmas" title="Puskesmas">Puskesmas</a> (Health Community Center) listed by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Indonesia)" title="Ministry of Health (Indonesia)">Ministry of Health of Indonesia</a>, which provide comprehensive healthcare and vaccination for the population in the sub-district level. Both traditional and modern health practices are employed. A data taken from <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) of 2013 shows that government health expenditures are about 3.1 percent of the total <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP).<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Israel">Health care in Israel</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Israel" title="Health in Israel">Health in Israel</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, the publicly funded medical system is universal and compulsory. In 2005, Israel spent 7.8% of GDP on health care, or US$1,533 per capita. Of that, approximately 66% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_system_in_Japan" title="Health care system in Japan">Health care system in Japan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Japan" title="Health in Japan">Health in Japan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, services are provided either through regional/national public hospitals or through private hospitals/clinics, and patients have universal access to any facility, though hospitals tend to charge higher for those without a referral.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Public health insurance covers most citizens/residents and pays 70% or more cost for each care and each prescribed drug. Patients are responsible for the remainder (upper limits apply). The monthly insurance premium is 0–50,000 JPY per household (scaled to annual income). Supplementary private health insurance is available only to cover the co-payments or non-covered costs, and usually makes a fixed payment per days in hospital or per surgery performed, rather than per actual expenditure. In 2005, Japan spent 8.2% of GDP on health care, or US$2,908 per capita. Of that, approximately 83% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jordan">Jordan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Jordan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Jordan" title="Health in Jordan">Health in Jordan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In comparison to most of its neighbors, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> has quite an advanced <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> system, although services remain highly concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a>. Government figures have put total health spending in 2002 at some 7.5 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">Gross domestic product</a> (GDP), while international health organizations place the figure even higher, at approximately 9.3 percent of GDP. The country's health care system is divided between public and private institutions. In the public sector, the Ministry of Health operates 1,245 primary health care centers and 27 hospitals, accounting for 37 percent of all hospital beds in the country; the military's Royal Medical Services runs 11 <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a>, providing 24 percent of all beds; and the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_University_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Jordan University Hospital">Jordan University Hospital</a> accounts for 3 percent of total beds in the country. The private sector provides 36 percent of all hospital beds, distributed among 56 hospitals. On 1 June 2007, Jordan Hospital (as the biggest private hospital) was the first general specialty hospital who gets the international accreditation (JCI). Treatment cost in Jordan hospitals is less than in other countries. <sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-139">[139]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Kazakhstan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Kazakhstan" title="Healthcare in Kazakhstan">Healthcare in Kazakhstan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In principle, health care is paid for by the government. However, <a href="/wiki/Bribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Bribes">bribes</a> often are necessary to obtain needed care. The quality of health care, which remained entirely under state control in 2006, has declined in the post-Soviet era because of insufficient funding and the loss of technical experts through emigration. Between 1989 and 2001, the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a> per 10,000 inhabitants fell by 15 percent, to 34.6, and the ratio of hospital beds per 10,000 inhabitants fell by 46 percent, to 74. By 2005 those indicators had recovered somewhat, to 55 and 77, respectively. Since 1991, health care has consistently lacked adequate government funding; in 2005 only 2.5 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> went for that purpose. A government health reform program aims to increase that figure to 4 percent in 2010. A compulsory <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> system has been in the planning stages for several years. Wages for health workers are extremely low, and equipment is in critically short supply. The main foreign source of medical equipment is Japan. Because of cost, the emphasis of treatment increasingly is on outpatient care instead of the hospital care preferred under the Soviet system. The health system is in crisis in rural areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> region, where health is most affected by <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated10_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated10-140">[140]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Malaysia" title="Healthcare in Malaysia">Healthcare in Malaysia</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Malaysia" title="Health in Malaysia">Health in Malaysia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health care in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> is divided into private and public sectors. Doctors are required to undergo a 2-year internship and perform 3 years of service with public hospitals throughout the nation, ensuring adequate coverage of medical needs for the general population. Foreign doctors are encouraged to apply for employment in Malaysia, especially if they are qualified to a higher level. </p>
      <p>Malaysian society places importance on the expansion and development of health care, putting 5% of the government social sector development budget into <a href="/wiki/Publicly_funded_health_care" title="Publicly funded health care">public health care</a> – an increase of more than 47% over the previous figure. This has meant an overall increase of more than RM 2 billion. With a rising and ageing population, the Government wishes to improve in many areas including the refurbishment of existing hospitals, building and equipping new hospitals, expansion of the number of polyclinics, and improvements in training and expansion of <a href="/wiki/Telehealth" title="Telehealth">telehealth</a>. Over the last couple of years they have increased their efforts to overhaul the systems and attract more foreign investment. </p>
      <p>There is still a shortage in the medical workforce, especially of highly trained specialists. As a result, certain medical care and treatment is available only in large cities. Recent efforts to bring many facilities to other towns have been hampered by lack of expertise to run the available equipment made ready by investments. </p>
      <p>The majority of <a href="/wiki/Private_hospital" title="Private hospital">private hospital</a> facilities are in urban areas and, unlike many of the public hospitals, are equipped with the latest diagnostic and imaging facilities. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_North_Korea" title="Health in North Korea">Health in North Korea</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> has a national medical service and health insurance system.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> As of 2000, some 99 percent of the population had access to <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>, and 100 percent had access to water, but water was not always <a href="/wiki/Potable" class="mw-redirect" title="Potable">potable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> Medical treatment is paid for by the state.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> In the past, there reportedly has been one doctor for every 700 inhabitants and one hospital bed for every 350 inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> Health expenditures in 2001 were 2.5 percent of gross domestic product, and 73 percent of health expenditures were made in the public sector.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> There were no reported human immuno-deficiency virus/acquired immune deficiency syndrome (<a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>) cases as of 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> However, it is estimated that between 500,000 and 3 million people died from <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> in the 1990s, and a 1998 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="World Food Program">World Food Program</a> report revealed that 60 percent of children suffered from <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, and 16 percent were acutely malnourished.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> UN statistics for the period 1999–2001 reveal that North Korea's daily per capita food supply was one of the lowest in Asia, exceeding only that of <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, and one of the lowest worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> Because of continuing economic problems, food shortages and chronic malnutrition prevail in the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-141">[141]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oman">Oman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Oman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Oman" title="Healthcare in Oman">Healthcare in Oman</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Oman" title="Health in Oman">Health in Oman</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Oman's healthcare system was ranked at number 8 by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization_ranking_of_health_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organization ranking of health systems">WHO health systems ranking</a> in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2000_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2000-131">[131]</a></sup> Universal healthcare (including prescriptions and dental care) is provided automatically to all citizens and also to expatriates working in the public sector by the Ministry of Health. Non-eligible individuals such as expatriates working in the private sector and foreign visitors can be treated in the government hospitals and clinics for a reasonable fee or they can opt for the more expensive private clinics and medical centres. The Ministry of Health also finances the treatment of citizens abroad if the required treatment is not available in Oman. The life expectancy in Oman as of 2007 was 71.6. It had 1.81 doctors per 1000 pop., 1.9 beds per 1000 pop. and an infant mortality rate of 9 per 1000 live births. Health expenditure accounts for 4.5% of government revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Pakistan" title="Healthcare in Pakistan">Healthcare in Pakistan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Pakistan" title="Health in Pakistan">Health in Pakistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Health_indicators" class="mw-redirect" title="Health indicators">health indicators</a>, health funding, and health and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> infrastructure are generally poor, particularly in rural areas. About 19 percent of the population is <a href="/wiki/Malnourished" class="mw-redirect" title="Malnourished">malnourished</a> – a higher rate than the 17 percent average for developing countries – and 30 percent of children under age five are malnourished. Leading causes of sickness and death include <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_infections" class="mw-redirect" title="Respiratory infections">respiratory infections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congenital_abnormalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Congenital abnormalities">congenital abnormalities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> estimates that in 2003 Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">human immunodeficiency virus</a> (HIV) prevalence rate was 0.1 percent among those 15–49, with an estimated 4,900 deaths from acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS). AIDS is a major health concern, and both the government and religious community are engaging in efforts to reduce its spread. In 2003 there were 68 physicians for every 100,000 persons in Pakistan. According to 2002 government statistics, there were 12,501 health institutions nationwide, including 4,590 dispensaries, 906 <a href="/wiki/Hospitals" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a> with a total of 80,665 hospital beds, and 550 rural health centers with a total of 8,840 beds. According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, Pakistan's <b>total health expenditures</b> amounted to 3.9 percent of gross domestic product (<a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>) in 2001, and per capita health expenditures were US$16. The government provided 24.4 percent of total health expenditures, with the remainder being entirely private, out-of-pocket expenses. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_the_Philippines" title="Health care in the Philippines">Health care in the Philippines</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_the_Philippines" title="Health in the Philippines">Health in the Philippines</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2000 the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> had about 95,000 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a>, or about 1 per 800 people. In 2001 there were about 1,700 <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a>, of which about 40 percent were government-run and 60 percent private, with a total of about 85,000 beds, or about one bed per 900 people. The leading causes of morbidity as of 2002 were <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular diseases">Cardiovascular diseases</a> account for more than 25 percent of all deaths. According to official estimates, 1,965 cases of <a href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">human immunodeficiency virus</a> (HIV) were reported in 2003, of which 636 had developed <a href="/wiki/Acquired_immune_deficiency_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Acquired immune deficiency syndrome">acquired immune deficiency syndrome</a> (AIDS). Other estimates state that there may have been as many as 9,400 people living with HIV/AIDS in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated18_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated18-143">[143]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Singapore" title="Healthcare in Singapore">Healthcare in Singapore</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Singapore" title="Health in Singapore">Health in Singapore</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Singapore" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Singapore">Health care in Singapore</a> is mainly under the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Singapore Government">Singapore Government</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Singapore)" title="Ministry of Health (Singapore)">Ministry of Health</a>. Singapore generally has an efficient and widespread system of health care. It implements a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system, and co-exists with private health care system. <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> rate: in 2006 the crude birth rate stood at 10.1 per 1000, and the crude death rate was also one of the lowest in the world at 4.3 per 1000. In 2006, the total fertility rate was only 1.26 children per woman, the 3rd lowest in the world and well below the 2.10 needed to replace the population. Singapore was ranked 6th in the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s ranking of the world's health systems in the year 2000. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> has a universal health care system where government ensures affordability, largely through compulsory savings and price controls, while the private sector provides most care. Overall spending on health care amounts to only 3% of annual GDP. Of that, 66% comes from private sources.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS-144">[144]</a></sup> Singapore currently has the lowest infant mortality rate in the world (equaled only by Iceland) and among the highest life expectancies from birth, according to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Singapore has "one of the most successful health care systems in the world, in terms of both efficiency in financing and the results achieved in community health outcomes," according to an analysis by global consulting firm <a href="/wiki/Watson_Wyatt_Worldwide" title="Watson Wyatt Worldwide">Watson Wyatt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_Wyatt_Singapore_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_Wyatt_Singapore-146">[146]</a></sup> Singapore's system uses a combination of compulsory savings from payroll deductions (funded by both employers and workers) a nationalized catastrophic health insurance plan, and government subsidies, as well as "actively regulating the supply and prices of health care services in the country" to keep costs in check; the specific features have been described as potentially a "very difficult system to replicate in many other countries." Many Singaporeans also have supplemental private health insurance (often provided by employers) for services not covered by the government's programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_Wyatt_Singapore_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_Wyatt_Singapore-146">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Singapore's well-established health care system comprises a total of 13 private hospitals, 10 public (government) hospitals and several specialist clinics, each specializing in and catering to different patient needs, at varying costs. </p>
      <p>Patients are free to choose the providers within the government or private health care delivery system and can walk in for a consultation at any private clinic or any government polyclinic. For emergency services, patients can go at any time to the 24-hour Accident &amp; Emergency Departments located in the government hospitals. </p>
      <p>Singapore's medical facilities are among the finest in the world, with well qualified doctors and dentists, many trained overseas. </p>
      <p>Singapore has <a href="/wiki/Medical_savings_account" title="Medical savings account">medical savings account</a> system known as <a href="/wiki/Medisave" title="Medisave">Medisave</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_South_Korea" title="Healthcare in South Korea">Healthcare in South Korea</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_South_Korea" title="Health in South Korea">Health in South Korea</a>
      </div> 
      <p>South Koreans have access to a universal healthcare safety net, although a significant portion of healthcare is privately funded. In 2015, South Korea ranked first in the OECD for healthcare access.[1] Satisfaction of healthcare has been consistently among the highest in the world – South Korea was rated as the second most efficient healthcare system by Bloomberg. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syria">Syria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Syria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Syria" title="Health in Syria">Health in Syria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Syria_Region" title="Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party – Syria Region">Syrian Ba'ath Party</a> has placed an emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a>, but funding levels have not been able to keep up with demand or maintain quality. Health expenditures reportedly accounted for 2.5 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) in 2001. Syria's health system is relatively decentralized and focuses on offering primary health care at three levels: village, district, and provincial. According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), in 1990 Syria had 41 general <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a> (33 public, 8 private), 152 specialized hospitals (16 public, 136 private), 391 rural health centers, 151 urban health centers, 79 rural health units, and 49 specialized health centers; <a href="/wiki/Hospital_bed" title="Hospital bed">hospital beds</a> totaled 13,164 (77 percent public, 23 percent private), or 11 beds per 10,000 inhabitants. The number of state hospital beds reportedly fell between 1995 and 2001, while the population had an 18 percent increase, but the opening of new hospitals in 2002 caused the number of hospital beds to double. WHO reported that in 1989 Syria had a total of 10,114 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a>, 3,362 <a href="/wiki/Dentist" title="Dentist">dentists</a>, and 14,816 <a href="/wiki/Nurse" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurse">nurses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Midwive" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwive">midwives</a>; in 1995 the rate of health professionals per 10,000 inhabitants was 10.9 physicians, 5.6 dentists, and 21.2 nurses and midwives. Despite overall improvements, Syria's health system exhibits significant regional disparities in the availability of health care, especially between urban and rural areas. The number of private hospitals and doctors increased by 41 percent between 1995 and 2001 as a result of growing demand and growing wealth in a small sector of society. Almost all private health facilities are located in large urban areas such as <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tartus" title="Tartus">Tartus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latakia" title="Latakia">Latakia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-147">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Taiwan.2C_Republic_of_China"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan,_Republic_of_China">Taiwan, Republic of China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Taiwan, Republic of China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Taiwan" title="Healthcare in Taiwan">Healthcare in Taiwan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Taiwan" title="Health in Taiwan">Health in Taiwan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The current health care system in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, known as National Health Insurance (NHI), was instituted in 1995. NHI is a single-payer compulsory social insurance plan which centralizes the disbursement of health care dollars. The system promises equal access to health care for all citizens, and the population coverage had reached 99% by the end of 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> NHI is mainly financed through premiums, which are based on the payroll tax, and is supplemented with out-of-pocket payments and direct government funding. In the initial stage, fee-for-service predominated for both public and private providers. Most health providers operate in the private sector and form a competitive market on the health delivery side. However, many health care providers took advantage of the system by offering unnecessary services to a larger number of patients and then billing the government. In the face of increasing loss and the need for cost containment, NHI changed the payment system from fee-for-service to a global budget, a kind of prospective payment system, in 2002. </p>
      <p>According to <a href="/wiki/T.R._Reid" class="mw-redirect" title="T.R. Reid">T.R. Reid</a>, Taiwan achieves "remarkable efficiency", costing ≈6 percent of GDP <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal coverage</a>; however, this underestimates the cost as it is not fully funded and the government is forced to borrow to make up the difference. "And frankly, the solution is fairly obvious: increase the spending a little, to maybe 8 percent of GDP. Of course, if Taiwan did that, it would still be spending less than half of what America spends."<sup id="cite_ref-trr_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trr-149">[149]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Thailand" title="Healthcare in Thailand">Healthcare in Thailand</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Thailand" title="Health in Thailand">Health in Thailand</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The majority of health care services in Thailand is delivered by the public sector, which includes 1,002 hospitals and 9,765 health stations. <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">Universal health care</a> is provided through three programs: the civil service welfare system for civil servants and their families, Social Security for private employees, and the Universal Coverage scheme theoretically available to all other Thai nationals. Some private hospitals are participants in these programs, though most are financed by patient self-payment and private insurance. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Public_Health_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Public Health (Thailand)">Ministry of Public Health</a> (MOPH) oversees national health policy and also operates most government health facilities. The National Health Security Office (NHSO) allocates funding through the Universal Coverage program. Other health-related government agencies include the Health System Research Institute (HSRI), Thai Health Promotion Foundation ("ThaiHealth"), National Health Commission Office (NHCO), and the Emergency Medical Institute of Thailand (EMIT). Although there have been national policies for decentralization, there has been resistance in implementing such changes and the MOPH still directly controls most aspects of health care. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Turkmenistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Turkmenistan" title="Healthcare in Turkmenistan">Healthcare in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Turkmenistan" title="Health in Turkmenistan">Health in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In the post-Soviet era, reduced funding has put the health system in poor condition. In 2002 <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> had 50 hospital beds per 10,000 population, less than half the number in 1996. Overall policy has targeted specialized inpatient facilities to the detriment of basic, outpatient care. Since the late 1990s, many rural facilities have closed, making care available principally in urban areas. President <a href="/wiki/Saparmurat_Niyazov" title="Saparmurat Niyazov">Niyazov</a>’s 2005 proposal to close all hospitals outside <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashgabat</a> intensified this trend. <a href="/wiki/Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Physicians">Physicians</a> are poorly trained, modern medical techniques are rarely used, and medications are in short supply. In 2004 Niyazov dismissed 15,000 medical professionals, exacerbating the shortage of personnel. In some cases, professionals have been replaced by military conscripts. Private health care is rare, as the state maintains a near monopoly. Government-paid public health care was abolished in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated15_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated15-150">[150]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Arab_Emirates">United Arab Emirates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: United Arab Emirates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Healthcare in the United Arab Emirates">Healthcare in the United Arab Emirates</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Health in the United Arab Emirates">Health in the United Arab Emirates</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Standards of health care are considered to be generally high in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, resulting from increased government spending during strong economic years. According to the UAE government, total expenditures on health care from 1996 to 2003 were US$436 million. According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, in 2004 total expenditures on health care constituted 2.9 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP), and the per capita expenditure for health care was US$497. Health care currently is government-paid only for UAE citizens. Effective January 2006, all residents of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a> are covered by a new comprehensive health insurance program; costs will be shared between employers and employees. The number of <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a> per 100,000 (annual average, 1990–99) is 181. The UAE now has 40 public hospitals, compared with only seven in 1970. The Ministry of Health is undertaking a multimillion-dollar program to expand health facilities – hospitals, medical centers, and a trauma center – in the seven emirates. A state-of-the-art general hospital has opened in Abu Dhabi with a projected bed capacity of 143, a trauma unit, and the first home health care program in the UAE. To attract wealthy UAE nationals and expatriates who traditionally have traveled abroad for serious medical care, Dubai is developing <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Healthcare_City" title="Dubai Healthcare City">Dubai Healthcare City</a>, a hospital free zone that will offer international-standard advanced private health care and provide an academic medical training center; completion is scheduled for 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated13_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated13-151">[151]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Uzbekistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Uzbekistan" title="Health in Uzbekistan">Health in Uzbekistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In the post-<a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> era, the quality of <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> has declined. Between 1992 and 2003, spending on health care and the ratio of hospital beds to population both decreased by nearly 50 percent, and Russian <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> in that decade deprived the health system of many practitioners. In 2004 Uzbekistan had 53 hospital beds per 10,000 population. Basic medical supplies such as disposable <a href="/wiki/Hypodermic_needle" title="Hypodermic needle">needles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anesthetics" class="mw-redirect" title="Anesthetics">anesthetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> are in very short supply. Although all citizens nominally are entitled to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Government-paid_health_care&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Government-paid health care (page does not exist)">free health care</a>, in the post-Soviet era <a href="/wiki/Bribery" title="Bribery">bribery</a> has become a common way to bypass the slow and limited service of the state system. In the early 2000s, policy has focused on improving primary health care facilities and cutting the cost of inpatient facilities. The state budget for 2006 allotted 11.1 percent to health expenditures, compared with 10.9 percent in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated12_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated12-152">[152]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Vietnam" title="Healthcare in Vietnam">Healthcare in Vietnam</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Vietnam" title="Health in Vietnam">Health in Vietnam</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The overall quality of <b>health in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></b> is regarded as good, as reflected by 2005 estimates of <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> (70.61 years) and infant mortality (25.95 per 1,000 live births). However, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> is still common in the provinces, and the life expectancy and <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rates are stagnating. In 2001 government spending on health care corresponded to just 0.9 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP). Government subsidies covered only about 20 percent of health care expenses, with the remaining 80 percent coming out of individuals’ own pockets.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-153">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1954 the government in the North established a public health system that reached down to the hamlet level. After reunification in 1976, this system was extended to the South. Beginning in the late 1980s, the quality of health care began to decline as a result of budgetary constraints, a shift of responsibility to the provinces, and the introduction of charges. Inadequate funding has led to delays in planned upgrades to <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> systems. As a result, almost half the population has no access to clean water, a deficiency that promotes such infectious diseases as <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>. Inadequate funding also has contributed to a shortage of <a href="/wiki/Nurses" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurses">nurses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midwives" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwives">midwives</a>, and hospital beds. In 2000 Vietnam had only 250,000 hospital beds, or 14.8 beds per 10,000 people, a very low ratio among Asian nations, according to the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yemen">Yemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Yemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Yemen" title="Health in Yemen">Health in Yemen</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Despite the significant progress <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> has made to expand and improve its health care system over the past decade, the system remains severely underdeveloped. Total expenditures on health care in 2002 constituted 3.7 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a>. In that same year, the per capita expenditure for health care was very low, as compared with other <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> countries – US$58 according to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> statistics and US$23 according to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, the number of <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a> in Yemen rose by an average of more than 7 percent between 1995 and 2000, but as of 2004 there were still only three doctors per 10,000 persons. In 2003 Yemen had only 0.6 hospital beds available per 1,000 persons.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated17_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated17-154">[154]</a></sup> Health care services are particularly scarce in rural areas; only 25 percent of rural areas are covered by health services, as compared with 80 percent of urban areas. <a href="/wiki/Emergency_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Emergency services">Emergency services</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ambulance" title="Ambulance">ambulance</a> service and <a href="/wiki/Blood_banks" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood banks">blood banks</a>, are non-existent. Most childhood deaths are caused by illnesses for which <a href="/wiki/Vaccines" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaccines">vaccines</a> exist or that are otherwise preventable. According to the <a href="/wiki/Joint_United_Nations_Programme_on_HIV/AIDS" title="Joint United Nations Programme on HIV/AIDS">Joint United Nations Programme on HIV/AIDS</a>, in 2003 an estimated 12,000 people in Yemen were living with <a href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus/acquired_immune_deficiency_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Human immunodeficiency virus/acquired immune deficiency syndrome">human immunodeficiency virus/acquired immune deficiency syndrome</a> (HIV/AIDS).<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Belgium" title="Healthcare in Belgium">Healthcare in Belgium</a>
      </div> 
      <p>As in most countries, the Belgian system divides itself into state and private, though fees are payable in both. A person must have adequate coverage through either the state or private insurance. In the state <i>mutuelle/mutualiteit</i> scheme a person has the ability to choose any doctor, clinic or hospital in any location without referral, according to the patient's needs in much the same way as with private insurance. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctors">Doctors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Doctors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>General practitioners can be found in private practices or attached to clinics and hospitals. A person is free to consult or register with any of their own choosing. Similarly with specialist consultants. Reimbursements are available for those with insurance, either private or public. If a patient is on a private scheme, or is uninsured, the fee is payable in full at the time of the appointment. The patient then brings, mails or deposits the receipt to his insurance mutuality which then immediately repays the amount. </p>
      <p>The majority of dentists in Belgium are private, though there are those who accept part-payment on state insurance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>As with general practitioners, patients can arrange to see a specialist of their choice at any hospital. Those going into hospital for a planned stay need to take personal care items (such as a towel, soap etc.) with them.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, the eleven big public hospitals are organized under the Iris association.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Complementary_health_care">Complementary health care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Complementary health care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The Ministry of Health recognizes <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteopathy" title="Osteopathy">osteopathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chiropractic" title="Chiropractic">chiropractic</a> as reimbursable alternative treatments. Reimbursement is possible only if the practitioner is registered as a qualified doctor. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_treatment">Emergency treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Emergency treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>If a call is made to the Emergency services using the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_telephone_number" title="Emergency telephone number">old emergency number (<b>100</b>)</a> or the <a href="/wiki/112_(emergency_telephone_number)" title="112 (emergency telephone number)">European telephone number (<b>112</b>)</a>, an ambulance will transport the patient to the nearest hospital or the best centre suited according to the needs of the patient, for example, a <a href="/wiki/Burn_center" title="Burn center">Specialist Burns Unit</a>. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacies">Pharmacies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Pharmacies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Pharmacies" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmacies">Pharmacies</a> are common in Belgium and are marked with a green cross on the street. There is a rota system for pharmacists to open outside of usual hours through the night.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_Insurance">Health Insurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Health Insurance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Health care insurance is a part of the Belgian social security system. To enroll, a person must first join a health insurance fund <i>mutuelle</i> (<i>mutualité</i>) or <i>ziekenfonds</i> (<i>mutualiteit</i>) for which an employer's certificate is required if the employer is to contribute to the cost. If employed a person's contributions is automatically deducted from salary. The employer will also pay a contribution. Health insurance funds will reimburse medical costs. The choice of mutual insurer is up to the individual. Most of them are affiliated to a religious or political institution but there is no real difference between them because reimbursement rates are fixed by the Belgian government.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Insurance funds do not always cover the full costs of treatment and typical reimbursement is between half to three-quarters of a typical doctors or specialists visit. A deciding factor here depends on one's job. From people who are unemployed or disabled, receiving other benefits or business-owners receive somewhat less. There is also a <i>"yearly maximum bill"</i> meaning that someone who has paid a certain amount to their doctor/hospital within the year does not have to make any further payment. From this point, any extra is returned from the patient's insurance. In general, the poor, even without reaching the necessary sum, do not pay anything.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Insured persons have a standardized credit card style SIS-card<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> which is needed in pharmacies and hospitals.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Bulgaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Bulgaria" title="Health in Bulgaria">Health in Bulgaria</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> began overall reform of its antiquated health system, inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communist</a> era, only in 1999. In the 1990s, private medical practices expanded somewhat, but most Bulgarians relied on communist-era public <a href="/wiki/Clinic" title="Clinic">clinics</a> while paying high prices for special care. During that period, national health indicators generally worsened as economic crises substantially decreased health funding. The subsequent health reform program has introduced mandatory employee health insurance through the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Insurance_Fund" title="National Health Insurance Fund">National Health Insurance Fund</a> (NHIF), which since 2000 has paid a gradually increasing portion of primary health care costs. Employees and employers pay an increasing, mandatory percentage of <a href="/wiki/Salary" title="Salary">salaries</a>, with the goal of gradually reducing state support of health care. Private health insurance plays only a supplementary role. The system also has been decentralized by making municipalities responsible for their own health care facilities, and by 2005 most primary care came from private <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a>. Pharmaceutical distribution also was decentralized.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-162">[162]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the early 2000s, the hospital system was reduced substantially to limit reliance on hospitals for routine care. Anticipated membership in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (2007) was a major motivation for this trend. Between 2002 and 2003, the number of hospital beds was reduced by 56 percent to 24,300. However, the pace of reduction slowed in the early 2000s; in 2004 some 258 hospitals were in operation, compared with the estimated optimal number of 140. Between 2002 and 2004, health care expenditures in the national budget increased from 3.8 percent to 4.3 percent, with the NHIF accounting for more than 60 percent of annual expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1990s, the quality of medical research and training decreased seriously because of low funding. In the early 2000s, the emphasis of medical and <a href="/wiki/Paramedic" title="Paramedic">paramedical</a> training, which was conducted in five medical schools, was preparation of primary-care personnel to overcome shortages resulting from the communist system's long-term emphasis on training specialists. Experts considered that Bulgaria had an adequate supply of <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a> but a shortage of other medical personnel. In 2000 Bulgaria had 3.4 doctors, 3.9 nurses, and 0.5 midwives per 1,000 population.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_84-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Denmark">Health care in Denmark</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Denmark's health care system has retained the same basic structure since the early 1970s. The administration of hospitals and personnel is dealt with by the Ministry of the Interior, while primary care facilities, health insurance, and community care are the responsibility of the Ministry of Social Affairs. Anyone can go to a physician for no fee and the public health system entitles each Dane to his/her own doctor. Expert medical/surgical aid is available, with a qualified nursing staff. Costs are borne by public authorities, but high taxes contribute to these costs. As of 1999, there were an estimated 3.4 physicians and 4.5 hospital beds per 1,000 people. The number of hospital beds, like that in other EU countries, has undergone a major decline since 1980, from around 40,000 to about 23,000 in 1998/99. Deinstitutionalization of psychiatric patients has contributed significantly to this trend. The ratio of doctors to population, by contrast, has increased during this period. </p>
      <p>The total fertility rate in 2000 was 1.7, while the maternal mortality rate was 10 per 100,000 live births as of 1998. Studies show that between 1980 and 1993, 63% of married women (ages 15 to 49) used contraception. As of 2002 cardiovascular diseases and cancer were the leading causes of death. Denmark's cancer rates were the highest in the European Union. In 1999, there were only 12 reported cases of tuberculosis per 100,000 people. As of 1999, the number of people living with HIV/AIDS was estimated at 4,300 and deaths from AIDS that year were estimated at less than 100. HIV prevalence was 0.17 per 100 adults. </p>
      <p>Danish citizens may choose between two systems of primary health care: medical care paid for by the government provided by a doctor whom the individual chooses for a year and by those specialists to whom the doctor refers the patient; or complete freedom of choice of any physician or specialist at any time, with state reimbursement of about two-thirds of the cost for medical bills paid directly by the patient. Most Danes opt for the former. All patients receive subsidies on pharmaceuticals and vital drugs; everyone must pay a share of dental bills. As of 1999, total health care expenditure was estimated at 8.4% of GDP. </p>
      <p>Responsibility for the public hospital service rests with county authorities. Counties form public hospital regions, each of which is allotted one or two larger hospitals with specialists and two to four smaller hospitals where medical treatment is practically totally paid for by the government. State-appointed medical health officers, responsible to the National Board of Health, are employed to advise local governments on health matters. Public health authorities have waged large-scale campaigns against tuberculosis, venereal diseases, diphtheria, and poliomyelitis. The government-paid guidance and assistance given to mothers of newborn children by public health nurses have resulted in a low infant mortality rate of 4 per 1,000 live births (2000). Medical treatment is government-paid up to school age, when government-paid school medical inspections begin. As of 1999, children up to one year of age were vaccinated against diphtheria, pertussis, and tetanus (99%) and measles (92%). In 2000, life expectancy at birth was 76 years for males and females. The overall death rate was 11 per 1,000 people in 1999. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estonia">Estonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: Estonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
