      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Estonia" title="Healthcare in Estonia">Healthcare in Estonia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Healthcare in Estonia is supervised by the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Ministry_of_Social_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Estonian Ministry of Social Affairs">Ministry of Social Affairs</a> and funded by general taxation through the National Health Service. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Finland" title="Healthcare in Finland">Healthcare in Finland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, public medical services at clinics and hospitals are run by the municipalities (local government) and are funded 78% by <a href="/wiki/Taxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxation">taxation</a>, 20% by patients through access charges, and by others 2%. Patient access charges are subject to annual caps. For example, GP visits are (€11 per visit with annual €33 cap), hospital outpatient treatment (€22 per visit), a hospital stay, including food, medical care and medicines (€26 per 24 hours, or €12 if in a psychiatric hospital). After a patient has spent €590 per year on public medical services, all treatment and medications thereafter are paid for by the government. Taxation funding is partly local and partly nationally based. Patients can claim re-imbursement of part of their prescription costs from <a href="/wiki/KELA_(Finnish_Social_Insurance)" class="mw-redirect" title="KELA (Finnish Social Insurance)">KELA</a>. Finland also has a much smaller private medical sector which accounts for about 14 percent of total health care spending. Only 8% of doctors choose to work in private practice, and some of these also choose to do some work in the public sector. Private sector patients can claim a contribution from KELA towards their private medical costs (including <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a>) if they choose to be treated in the more expensive private sector, or they can join private insurance funds. However, private sector health care is mainly in the primary care sector. There are virtually no private hospitals, the main hospitals being either municipally owned (funded from local taxes) or run by the teaching universities (funded jointly by the municipalities and the national government). In 2005, Finland spent 7.5% of GDP on health care, or US$2,824 per capita. Of that, approximately 78% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_France" title="Health care in France">Health care in France</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In its <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization_ranking_of_health_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organization ranking of health systems">2000 assessment of world health systems</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> found that France provided the "best overall health care" in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2000_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2000-131">[131]</a></sup> In 2005, France spent 11.2% of GDP on health care, or US$3,926 per capita. Of that, approximately 80% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, most doctors remain in private practice; there are both <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_hospital" title="Public hospital">public</a> <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a>. Social Security consists of several public organizations, distinct from the state government, with separate budgets that refunds patients for care in both private and public facilities. It generally refunds patients 70% of most health care costs, and 100% in case of costly or long-term ailments. Supplemental coverage may be bought from private insurers, most of them <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonprofit">nonprofit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_insurer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutual insurer">mutual insurers</a>, to the point that the word "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Mutuelle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mutuelle (page does not exist)">mutuelle</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutuelle_de_sant%C3%A9_en_France" class="extiw" title="fr:Mutuelle de santé en France">fr</a>]</sup>" (mutual) has come to be a synonym of supplemental private insurer in common language. </p>
      <p>Until recently, social security coverage was restricted to those who contributed to social security (generally, workers, unemployed or retirees), excluding some few poor segments of the population; the government of <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Jospin" title="Lionel Jospin">Lionel Jospin</a> put into place the "universal health coverage" allowing the entire French population to benefit from Health care. In some systems, patients can also take private health insurance but choose to receive care at public hospitals, if allowed by the private <a href="/wiki/Insurer" class="mw-redirect" title="Insurer">insurer</a>. For serious illness, regardless of the insurance regime, the national health system will assume the cost of long-term remedial treatment. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=74" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Germany" title="Health in Germany">Health in Germany</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:UniKlinik_(Freiburg)_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg/220px-UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg/330px-UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg/440px-UniKlinik_%28Freiburg%29_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="2742"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:UniKlinik_(Freiburg)_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/University_Medical_Center_Freiburg" title="University Medical Center Freiburg">University Medical Center Freiburg</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> has a universal multi-payer system with two main types of health insurance: Public "law-enforced" health insurance (Gesetzliche Krankenversicherung), and private health insurance (Private Krankenversicherung).<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Those who make below a certain income must use the public health insurance. Those are compulsorily insured (Pflichtversichert). Private health insurance is only available to freelancers and high earners.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-165">[165]</a></sup> Those are voluntarily insured (freiwillig versichert). Employers pay for half of their employees' health insurance contributions, while the self-employed must pay the full contribution themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-165">[165]</a></sup> Provider compensation rates are negotiated in complex <a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporatist</a> social bargaining among specified autonomously organized interest groups (e.g. physicians' associations) at the level of <a href="/wiki/States_of_Germany" title="States of Germany">federal states</a> (Länder). Public health insurers are mandated to provide a wide range of coverage. They can only refuse coverage to the privately insured who are self-employed, or high earners.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-165">[165]</a></sup> Small numbers of persons are covered by tax-funded government employee insurance or social welfare insurance. Persons with incomes above the prescribed compulsory insurance level may decide to remain in the sickness fund system, which a majority do, or opt out &amp; take private insurance. Private supplementary insurance to the sickness funds of various sorts is available. </p>
      <p>In 2005, Germany spent 10.7% of GDP on health care, or US$3,628 per capita. Of that, approximately 77% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=75" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Greece">Health care in Greece</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in Greece">Greek healthcare system</a> is <a href="/wiki/Universal_healthcare" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal healthcare">universal</a> and is ranked as one of the best in the world. In a 2000 <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> report it was ranked 14th in the overall assessment and 11th at quality of service, surpassing countries such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (18th) and <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (25th).<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_report_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_report-166">[166]</a></sup> In 2010 there were 131 hospitals with 35,000 beds in the country, but on 1 July 2011 the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_for_Health_and_Social_Solidarity_(Greece)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry for Health and Social Solidarity (Greece)">Ministry for Health and Social Solidarity</a> announced its proposal to shorten the number to 83 hospitals with 33,000 beds.<sup id="cite_ref-Health_Reform_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Health_Reform-167">[167]</a></sup> Greece's healthcare expenditures as a percentage of <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> were 9.6% in 2007 according to a 2011 <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> report, just above the OECD average of 9.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-168">[168]</a></sup> The country has the largest number of doctors-to-population ratio of any OECD country.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">Life expectancy</a> in Greece is 80.3 years, above the OECD average of 79.5.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-168">[168]</a></sup> and among the highest in the world. The same OECD report showed that Greece had the largest percentage of adult daily smokers of any of the 34 <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> members.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_168-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-168">[168]</a></sup> The country's obesity rate is 18.1%, which is above the OECD average of 15.1% but considerably below the American rate of 27.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_168-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-168">[168]</a></sup> In 2008 Greece had the highest rate of perceived good health in the OECD, at 98.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_Health_Status_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_Health_Status-169">[169]</a></sup> Infant mortality is one of the lowest in the <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed world</a> with a rate of 3.1 deaths/1000 live births.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iceland">Iceland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=76" title="Edit section: Iceland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Iceland" title="Healthcare in Iceland">Healthcare in Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Healthcare in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> is universal. The healthcare system is largely paid for by taxes (85%) and to some extent by service fees (15%) and is administrated by the Ministry of Welfare. A considerable portion of government spending is assigned to healthcare. There is almost no private health insurance in Iceland and no private hospitals. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=77" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Healthcare in the Republic of Ireland">Healthcare in the Republic of Ireland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>All persons resident in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> are entitled to health care through the public health care system, which is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Health_Service_Executive" title="Health Service Executive">Health Service Executive</a> and funded by general taxation. A person may be required to pay a subsidised fee for certain health care received; this depends on income, age, illness or disability. All maternity services are however paid for by the government, as well as health care of infants under 6 months of age. Emergency care is provided at a cost of €120 for a visit to a hospital Emergency Department. </p>
      <p>However, the poor quality of public healthcare has led to a large reliance on private health insurance; over 45% of Republic of Ireland citizens have private cover.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The Irish healthcare system is often described as being "<a href="/wiki/Two-tier_healthcare" title="Two-tier healthcare">two-tier</a>" or having a "public–private mix."<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> A proposed reform, known as <a href="/wiki/Sl%C3%A1intecare" title="Sláintecare">Sláintecare</a>, is being planned; it would provide universal healthcare on the model of the British NHS or other European systems.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=78" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Italy" title="Healthcare in Italy">Healthcare in Italy</a>
      </div> 
      <p>According to <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization_ranking_of_health_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organization ranking of health systems">WHO in 2000</a>, Italy had the world's "second overall best" healthcare system in the world, coming after <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and surpassing <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1978 <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> adopted a tax-funded <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system called "National Health Service" (in <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Servizio_Sanitario_Nazionale" class="mw-redirect" title="Servizio Sanitario Nazionale">Servizio Sanitario Nazionale</a></i>), which was closely modeled on the British system. The SSN covers general practice (distinct between adult and pediatric practice), outpatient and inpatient treatments, and the cost of most (but not all) drugs and sanitary ware.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The government sets LEA (fundamental levels of care, <i>Livelli essenziali di assistenza</i> in Italian) which cover all necessary treatments, which the state must guarantee to all, paid for by the government, or for a "ticket", a share of the costs (but various categories are exempted).<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The public system has also the duty of prevention at place of work and in the general environment. A private sector also exists, with a minority role in medicine but a principal role in <a href="/wiki/Dental_health" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental health">dental health</a>, as most people prefer private dental services. </p>
      <p>In Italy the public system has the unique feature of paying general practitioners a fee <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> per year, a salary system, that does not reward repeat visits, testing, and referrals.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> While there is a paucity of nurses, Italy has one of the highest doctor per capita ratios at 3.9 <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a> per 1,000 patients.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> In 2005, Italy spent 8.9% of GDP on health care, or US$2,714 per capita. Of that, approximately 76% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=79" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_the_Netherlands" title="Health in the Netherlands">Health in the Netherlands</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Health care in the Netherlands">Health care in the Netherlands</a>, has since January 2006 been provided by a system of compulsory insurance backed by a <a href="/wiki/Risk_equalization" title="Risk equalization">risk equalization</a> program so that the insured are not penalized for their age or health status. This is meant to encourage competition between health care providers and insurers. Children under 18 are insured by the government, and special assistance is available to those with limited incomes. In 2005, the Netherlands spent 9.2% of GDP on health care, or US$3,560 per capita. Of that, approximately 65% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=80" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Norway" title="Healthcare in Norway">Healthcare in Norway</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Norway has a government run and government financed universal health care system, covering physical and mental health for all and dental health for children under the age of 16. Hospitals are paid by the state and doctor visit fees are capped at a fairly low rate. Short-term prescriptions for medication are market price, but long-term prescriptions, defined as more than three months a year, are eligible for a large discount. In addition, a yearly cap applies for people with high medical expenses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Some health care is private. For example, most adults use private dental care, whereas the public system only treats people, for a normal fee, when they have free capacity. Health-related plastic surgery (like burn damage) is covered by the public system, while cosmetic surgery in general is private. There are a number of private psychologists, there are also some private general practice doctors and specialists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Public health care is financed by a special-purpose income tax on the order of 8–11%, loosely translated as "public benefits fee" (Norwegian: "trygdeavgift og Folketrygden"). This can be considered a mandatory public insurance, covering not only health care but also loss of income during sick leave, public pension, unemployment benefits, benefits for single parents and a few others. The system is supposed to be self-financing from the taxes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Norwegian citizens living in Norway are automatically covered, even if they never had taxable income. Norwegian citizens living and working abroad (taxable elsewhere and therefore not paying the "public benefits fee" to Norway) are covered for up to one year after they move abroad, and must pay an estimated market cost for public health care services. Non-citizens such as foreign visitors are covered in full.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>According to WHO, total health care expenditure in 2005 was 9% of GDP and paid 84% by government, 15% by private out-of-pocket and ≈1% by other private sources.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS-144">[144]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=81" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Poland" title="Health care in Poland">Health care in Poland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, healthcare is delivered through a publicly funded healthcare system, which is free for all citizens, and this is enshrined in Article 68 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Poland" title="Constitution of Poland">Constitution of Poland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Poland's expenditure on healthcare was 6.7% of GDP in 2012 or $900 per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The public spending rate for Poland in 2012 was 72% – in-line with the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> A number of private medical complexes also complement public healthcare institutions nationwide. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romania">Romania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=82" title="Edit section: Romania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Romania" title="Health in Romania">Health in Romania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Health care public system has been improved but it is still poor by European standards, and access is limited in rural areas. In 2007 health expenditures were equal to 3.9 percent of gross domestic product. In 2007 there were 2.2 physicians and 6.4 hospital beds per 1,000 people. The system is funded by the National Health Care Insurance Fund, to which employers and employees make mandatory contributions. Private health care system has developed slowly but now consists of 22 private hospitals and more than 240 clinics.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-bribes_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bribes-187">[187]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=83" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Russia" title="Healthcare in Russia">Healthcare in Russia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">

      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg/220px-%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg/330px-%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg/440px-%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BC%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Terminals for the booking (<a href="/wiki/EMIAS" title="EMIAS">EMIAS</a>) of polyclinic visits in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> using the new OMS insurance policy, the old OMS card or special <a href="/wiki/ATM_card" title="ATM card">ATM cards</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Russia in 1991-1993 has changed to a mixed model of health care. Article 41 of the 1993 constitution confirmed a citizen's right to healthcare and medical assistance free of charge in state and municipal health establishments.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Private clinics are separate system and it is funded by separate payments of their clients or by private insurance companies via paid voluntary health insurance (known as ДМС - DMS). Only paid clinics allow all types of anonymous medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-gosuslugi_1_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gosuslugi_1-190">[190]</a></sup> State and municipal health establishments also have the right to provide paid services to any person or in addition to free services. </p>
      <p>In all life-threatening cases, <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_services" title="Emergency medical services">emergency medical services</a>, including ambulance vehicles, hospitalization, surgery, etc, are completely free of charge and any documents and name/surname are not required.<sup id="cite_ref-gosluslugi_1_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gosluslugi_1-191">[191]</a></sup> According to the law "on the basics of health protection of citizens", emergency assistance is provided by a medical organization and a medical employee immediately and free of charge. Refusal to provide it is not allowed. If a medical organization participates in the program of state guarantees of free medical care, it is not allowed to refuse to provide medical care and charge fees.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> In both of the above cases, a medical employee and a medical organization can be punished under the Criminal Code if the patient has suffered "medium harm" or higher as a result of the lack of aid.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>State and municipal health establishments and their workers are funded by multiple sources. The system of compulsory medical insurance (обязательное медицинское страхование, ОМС - obyzatel'noye meditsinskoye strakhovaniye, OMS) is funded by <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_Russia#Federal_taxes" title="Taxation in Russia">general taxes</a> of individuals and companies via federal, regional and municipal budgets and by employer's additional quasi-tax obligatory payments (officially called "insurance contributions" but collected by <a href="/wiki/Federal_Tax_Service_(Russia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Tax Service (Russia)">Tax Service</a>) via the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Compulsory_Medical_Insurance_Fund_(Russia)" title="Federal Compulsory Medical Insurance Fund (Russia)">Federal Compulsory Medical Insurance Fund</a> and Territorial Funds of Russian regions.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Medical aid in state and municipal health establishments is available for free to all citizens as well as to foreign permanent residents, foreign temporary residents, stateless persons and refugees regardless of their income or employment status, in all cases with mandatory and free-of-charge pre-conclusion of a contract, confirmed by OMS insurance policy (полис ОМС - polis OMS), with one of the private insurance companies decided to participate in the OMS system.<sup id="cite_ref-mos.ru_1_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mos.ru_1-195">[195]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Internal_passport_of_Russia" title="Internal passport of Russia">Internal passport of Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/SNILS_(Russia)" title="SNILS (Russia)">Individual insurance account number</a> are required for Russian citizens. <a href="/wiki/Identity_document" title="Identity document">Identity document</a> and legal status in Russia are required for foreigners and stateless persons.<sup id="cite_ref-mos.ru_1_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mos.ru_1-195">[195]</a></sup> Lack of legal status for foreigners and military service in the Russian Armed Forces for Russian citizens are the only reasons for refusal to obtain or use OMS insurance policy provided that it is not allowed to have two OMS insurance policies.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> On 1 January 2018, 146.3 million persons were in the OMS system, including 66.4 mln employed and 79.9 mln unemployed persons<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> (the total population of Russia on 1 January 2018 was 146 880 432 persons). </p>
      <p>OMS insurance policy is a document confirming the right to receive free medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-mos.ru_1_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mos.ru_1-195">[195]</a></sup> OMS insurance policy or its details are required when applying to state and municipal health establishments. If a person has OMS insurance policy, but does not have it with him, it is not possible to refuse him medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-gosuslugi_1_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gosuslugi_1-190">[190]</a></sup> A person must choose a permanent establishment for medical care. Health establishment can refuse only in case of overcrowding.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Basic services are available for free throughout the country while regional list of services is available for free only in a <a href="/wiki/Resident_registration_in_Russia" title="Resident registration in Russia">region of permanent residence</a>. In some cases, free treatment in private clinics (that decided to participate in the OMS system, in very limited cases since this is one of the recent changes to the system), free dental prosthetics and free medications are available through the OMS system.<sup id="cite_ref-gosuslugi_1_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gosuslugi_1-190">[190]</a></sup> Introduction in 1993 reform of new free market providers in addition to the state-run institutions intended to promote both efficiency and patient choice. A purchaser-provider split help facilitate the restructuring of care, as resources would migrate to where there was greatest demand, reduce the excess capacity in the hospital sector and stimulate the development of primary care. Russian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> announced a new large-scale health care reform in 2011 and pledged to allocate more than 300 billion rubles ($10 billion) in the next few years to improve health care in the country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He also said that obligatory medical insurance tax paid by companies will increase from current 3.1% to 5.1% starting from 2011.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=84" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Sweden" title="Health in Sweden">Health in Sweden</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg/220px-Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg/330px-Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg/440px-Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1213" data-file-height="724"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Karolinska.sthlm.2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Karolinska university hospital in Stockholm (2006)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> public health system is funded through taxes levied by the <a href="/wiki/County_Councils_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="County Councils of Sweden">county councils</a>, but partly run by private companies. There is a fixed charge of SEK 150 (US$21) for each visit to a doctor or a hospital but some may vary depending on the business itself and cause of admission &amp; desired service whereas prices can vary up to SEK 350 (US$52). Healthcare services that are accepted by the Swedish Board of Health (hälsovårdsnämnden) have "safe net" limits for visitors placed upon them to a maximum SEK 800 (US$111) per year along with prescription medicine from those clinics are also limited to 1,800 SEK (US$249) per year. Government-paid dental care for children under 21 years old is included in the system, and dental care for grown-ups is to a small extent subsidised by it. Sweden also has a smaller private health care sector, mainly in larger cities or as centers for preventive health care financed by employers. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=85" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Switzerland" title="Healthcare in Switzerland">Healthcare in Switzerland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg/200px-Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg/300px-Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg/400px-Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Spaceship-style_Hospital_in_Zurich.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A private hospital in <a href="/wiki/Zurich,_Switzerland" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich, Switzerland">Zurich, Switzerland</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, compulsory <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> covers the costs of medical treatment and hospitalization of the insured. The Swiss healthcare system is a combination of public, subsidized private and totally private healthcare providers, where the insured person has full freedom of choice among the providers in his region. Insurance companies independently set their price points for different age groups, but are forbidden from setting prices based on health risk. In 2000, Switzerland topped all European countries’ health care expenditure when calculated as per capita expenditure in US dollar purchasing parity terms.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Swiss health care system was the last for-profit system in Europe. In the 1990s, after the private carriers began to deny coverage for pre-existing conditions – and when the uninsured population of Switzerland reached 5% – the Swiss held a referendum (1995) and adopted their present system. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey">Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=86" title="Edit section: Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare in Turkey">Healthcare in Turkey</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg/200px-Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg/300px-Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg/400px-Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="395"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_Memorial_Hastanesi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A hospital in <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0stanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="İstanbul">İstanbul</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Sosyal_G%C3%BCvenlik_Kurumu" class="mw-redirect" title="Sosyal Güvenlik Kurumu">Sosyal Güvenlik Kurumu</a>
      </div> 
      <p><b>Health care in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></b> used to be dominated by a centralized state system run by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Turkey)" title="Ministry of Health (Turkey)">Ministry of Health</a>. In 2003 the government introduced a sweeping health reform program aimed at increasing the ratio of private to state health provision and making health care available to a larger share of the population. Information from the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Statistical_Institute" title="Turkish Statistical Institute">Turkish Statistical Institute</a> states that 76.3 billions of <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Lira" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Lira">Turkish Liras</a> are being spent in healthcare, with 79.6% of funding coming from the <a href="/wiki/Sosyal_G%C3%BCvenlik_Kurumu" class="mw-redirect" title="Sosyal Güvenlik Kurumu">Sosyal Güvenlik Kurumu</a> and most of the remainder (15.4%) coming from out-of-pocket payments.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> There are 27.954 medical institutions, one doctor for 587 people and 2.54 beds for 1000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=87" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Health care in the United Kingdom">Health care in the United Kingdom</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The four <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a> have separate but co-operating public health care systems that were created in 1948: in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> the public health system is known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> it is known as <a href="/wiki/NHS_Scotland" title="NHS Scotland">NHS Scotland</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> as <a href="/wiki/NHS_Wales" title="NHS Wales">NHS Wales (GIG Cymru)</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> it is called <a href="/wiki/Health_and_Social_Care_in_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Health and Social Care in Northern Ireland">Health and Social Care in Northern Ireland</a>. All four provide state-paid healthcare to all UK residents, paid for from general taxation. Though the public systems dominate, private health care and a wide variety of alternative and complementary treatments are available for those who have private health insurance or are willing to pay directly themselves. </p>
      <p>One difference between the four public health care systems is the patient cost for prescriptions. Wales, Northern Ireland and Scotland have recently abolished, or are in the process of abolishing, all prescription charges, while England (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control_pills" class="mw-redirect" title="Birth control pills">birth control pills</a>, which are paid for by the state) continues to charge patients who are between 18 and 60 years old a fixed prescription fee of £9.15 per item or yearly prepayment of £105.90, unless they are exempt because of certain medical conditions (including cancer) or are on low income.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Since health care delivery is a <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved matter</a>, considerable differences are developing between the systems in each of the countries.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=88" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=89" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Australia" title="Health care in Australia">Health care in Australia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> the current system, known as <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(Australia)" title="Medicare (Australia)">Medicare</a>, was instituted in 1984. It coexists with a private health system. All legal permanent residents are entitled to government-paid public hospital care. Treatment by private doctors is also paid by the government when the doctor direct bills the Health Department (Bulk Billing). Medicare is funded partly by a 1.5% income tax levy (with exceptions for low-income earners), but mostly out of general revenue. An additional levy of 1% is imposed on high-income earners without private health insurance. There is a means tested 30% subsidy on private health insurance. As well as Medicare, there is a separate <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_Benefits_Scheme" title="Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme">Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme</a> under which listing and a government subsidy is dependent on expert evaluation of the comparative cost-effectiveness of new pharmaceuticals. In 2005, Australia spent 8.8% of <a href="/wiki/Gross_Domestic_Product" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross Domestic Product">GDP</a> on health care, or US$3,181 per capita. Of that, approximately 67% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=90" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_New_Zealand" title="Health care in New Zealand">Health care in New Zealand</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> hospitals are public and treat citizens or permanent residents, with the fees paid by the government, and are managed by <a href="/wiki/District_Health_Boards_(New_Zealand)" class="mw-redirect" title="District Health Boards (New Zealand)">District Health Boards</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Labour_Party" title="New Zealand Labour Party">Labour</a> coalition governments (1999–2008), there were plans to make <a href="/wiki/Primary_health_care" title="Primary health care">primary health care</a> available with charges paid for by the government. At present government subsidies exist in health care. The cost of visiting a GP ranges from government-paid to $45.00 for children and from government-paid to $75.00 for adults under the current subsidies. This system is funded by taxes. The New Zealand government agency <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_Management_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical Management Agency">Pharmac</a> subsidizes certain pharmaceuticals depending upon their category. Co-payments exist, however these are lower if the user has a <i>Community Services Card</i> or <i>High User Health Card</i>. In 2005, New Zealand spent 8.9% of GDP on health care, or US$2,403 per capita. Of that, approximately 77% was government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOSIS_spending_85-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOSIS_spending-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_care_systems_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=91" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
